	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Received Pronunciation</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<table role="presentation" class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA"><img alt="[]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/30px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png" decoding="async" width="30" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/45px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/60px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="75" data-file-height="100" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phonetic symbols.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA#Rendering_issues" title="Help:IPA">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>&#32;instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Received Pronunciation</b> (<b>RP</b>), commonly called <b>BBC English</b> and <b>Standard British pronunciation</b> or <b>Southern British pronunciation</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-jwl_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jwl-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> is an <a href="/wiki/Accent_(dialect)" class="mw-redirect" title="Accent (dialect)">accent</a> of <a href="/wiki/Standard_English" title="Standard English">Standard English</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and is defined in the <i><a href="/wiki/Concise_Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Concise Oxford English Dictionary">Concise Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> as "the standard accent of English as spoken in the <a href="/wiki/South_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="South of England">south of England</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearsall1999xiv_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearsall1999xiv-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> although it can be heard from native speakers throughout England and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells2008xiv_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells2008xiv-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Trudgill" title="Peter Trudgill">Peter Trudgill</a> estimated in 1974 that 3 per cent of people in Britain were RP speakers,<sup id="cite_ref-trudgillestimate_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trudgillestimate-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> but this rough estimate has been questioned by the phonetician <a href="/wiki/Jack_Windsor_Lewis" title="Jack Windsor Lewis">J. Windsor Lewis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>  Clive Upton notes higher estimates of 5% (Romaine, 2000) and 10% (Wells, 1982) but refers to all these as "guesstimates" that are not based on robust research.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Formerly colloquially called "the King's English", RP enjoys <a href="/wiki/Prestige_(sociolinguistics)" title="Prestige (sociolinguistics)">high social prestige</a> in Britain,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHudson1981337_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHudson1981337-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> being thought of as the accent of those with power, money, and influence, though it may be perceived negatively by some as being associated with undeserved privilege.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcArthur200243_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcArthur200243-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Since the 1960s, a greater permissiveness toward regional English varieties has taken hold in education.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFishman1977319_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFishman1977319-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The study of RP is concerned exclusively with pronunciation, whereas Standard English, the Queen's English, Oxford English, and BBC English are also concerned with matters such as grammar, vocabulary, and style.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The introduction of the term <i>Received Pronunciation</i> is usually credited to the British phonetician <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Jones_(phonetician)" title="Daniel Jones (phonetician)">Daniel Jones</a>. In the first edition of the <i><a href="/wiki/English_Pronouncing_Dictionary" title="English Pronouncing Dictionary">English Pronouncing Dictionary</a></i> (1917), he named the accent "Public School Pronunciation" ("<a href="/wiki/Public_school_(United_Kingdom)" title="Public school (United Kingdom)">public</a>" being what Americans would term "private"), but for the second edition in 1926, he wrote, "In what follows I call it Received Pronunciation, for want of a better term."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1926ix_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1926ix-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> However, the term had actually been used much earlier by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Stephen_Du_Ponceau" title="Peter Stephen Du Ponceau">P. S. Du Ponceau</a> in 1818.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuPonceau1818259_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuPonceau1818259-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> A similar term, <i>received standard,</i> was coined by <a href="/wiki/H._C._Wyld" class="mw-redirect" title="H. C. Wyld">Henry C. K. Wyld</a> in 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyld192723_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyld192723-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The early phonetician <a href="/wiki/Alexander_John_Ellis" title="Alexander John Ellis">Alexander John Ellis</a> used both terms interchangeably but with a much broader definition than Daniel Jones, having said "there is no such thing as a uniform eduction pron. of English, and rp. and rs. is a variable quantity differing from individual to individual, although all its varieties are 'received', understood and mainly unnoticed".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis18693_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis18693-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <i><a href="/wiki/Fowler%27s_Modern_English_Usage" class="mw-redirect" title="Fowler&#39;s Modern English Usage">Fowler's Modern English Usage</a></i> (1965), the correct term is "'the Received Pronunciation'. The word 'received' conveys its original meaning of 'accepted' or 'approved', as in '<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/received#Adjective" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:received">received</a> wisdom'."<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>RP is often believed to be based on the accents of <a href="/wiki/Southern_England" title="Southern England">southern England</a>, but it actually has most in common with the <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">Early Modern English</a> dialects of the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands" title="East Midlands">East Midlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> This was the most populated and most prosperous area of England during the 14th and 15th centuries. By the end of the 15th century, "Standard English" was established in the City of London.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal200354–55_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal200354–55-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2005243–244_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2005243–244-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_names">Alternative names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Alternative names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some linguists have used the term "RP" while expressing reservations about its suitability.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECruttenden200877–80_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECruttenden200877–80-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkins200013–16_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkins200013–16-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982117_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982117-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The Cambridge-published <i>English Pronouncing Dictionary</i> (aimed at those learning English as a foreign language) uses the phrase "BBC Pronunciation" on the basis that the name "Received Pronunciation" is "archaic" and that <a href="/wiki/BBC_news" class="mw-redirect" title="BBC news">BBC news</a> presenters no longer suggest high social class and privilege to their listeners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2011vi_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2011vi-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Other writers have also used the name "BBC Pronunciation".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELadefoged2004_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELadefoged2004-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgill1999_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgill1999-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The phonetician <a href="/wiki/Jack_Windsor_Lewis" title="Jack Windsor Lewis">Jack Windsor Lewis</a> frequently criticises the name "Received Pronunciation" in his blog: he has called it "invidious",<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> a "ridiculously archaic, parochial and question-begging term"<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and noted that American scholars find the term "quite curious".<sup id="cite_ref-jwl_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jwl-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> He used the term "General British" (to parallel "<a href="/wiki/General_American" class="mw-redirect" title="General American">General American</a>") in his 1970s publication of <i>A Concise Pronouncing Dictionary of American and British English</i> and in subsequent publications.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Beverley Collins and Inger Mees use the term "Non-Regional Pronunciation" for what is often otherwise called RP, and reserve the term "Received Pronunciation" for the "upper-class speech of the twentieth century".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollinsMees20033–4_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollinsMees20033–4-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Received Pronunciation has sometimes been called "Oxford English", as it used to be the accent of most members of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Handbook of the International Phonetic Association</i> uses the name "Standard Southern British". Page 4 reads:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Standard Southern British (where 'Standard' should not be taken as implying a value judgment of 'correctness') is the modern equivalent of what has been called 'Received Pronunciation' ('RP'). It is an accent of the south east of England which operates as a prestige norm there and (to varying degrees) in other parts of the British Isles and beyond.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInternational_Phonetic_Association19994_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInternational_Phonetic_Association19994-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>In her book <i>Kipling's English History</i> (1974) <a href="/wiki/Marghanita_Laski" title="Marghanita Laski">Marghanita Laski</a> refers to this accent as "gentry". "What the Producer and I tried to do was to have each poem spoken in the dialect that was, so far as we could tell, ringing in Kipling's ears when he wrote it. Sometimes the dialect is most appropriately, Gentry. More often, it isn't."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-varieties">Sub-varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Sub-varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Faced with the difficulty of defining RP, some researchers have tried to distinguish between different sub-varieties:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFGimson1980">Gimson (1980)</a> proposed Conservative, General, and Advanced; <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Received_Pronunciation" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative Received Pronunciation">Conservative RP</a> referred to a traditional accent associated with older speakers with certain social backgrounds; General RP was considered neutral regarding age, occupation or lifestyle of the speaker; and Advanced RP referred to speech of a younger generation of speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmitt2007323_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmitt2007323-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Later editions (e.g., Gimson 2008) use the terms General, Refined and Regional.</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFWells1982">Wells (1982)</a> refers to "mainstream RP" and "<a href="/wiki/U_and_non-U_English" title="U and non-U English">U-RP</a>"; he suggests that Gimson's categories of Conservative and Advanced RP referred to the U-RP of the old and young respectively. However, Wells stated, "It is difficult to separate stereotype from reality" with U-RP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Writing on his blog in February 2013, Wells wrote, "If only a very small percentage of English people speak RP, as Trudgill et al claim, then the percentage speaking U-RP is vanishingly small" and "If I were redoing it today, I think I'd drop all mention of 'U-RP'".<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Clive_Upton" title="Clive Upton">Upton</a> distinguishes between RP (which he equates with Wells's "mainstream RP"), Traditional RP (after Ramsaran 1990), and an even older version which he identifies with Cruttenden's "Refined RP".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>An article on the website of the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a> refers to <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Received_Pronunciation" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative Received Pronunciation">Conservative</a>, Mainstream and Contemporary RP.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Usage">Usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Teachers often promote the modern RP accent to non-native speakers learning <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Non-RP Britons abroad may modify their pronunciation to something closer to Received Pronunciation to allow better understanding by people unfamiliar with the diversity of British accents. They may also modify their <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a> to approach those of <a href="/wiki/Standard_English" title="Standard English">Standard English</a> for the same reason. RP serves as the standard for English in most books on general <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetics</a>, and most dictionaries published in the United Kingdom use RP in their pronunciation schemes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Most British voices in apps like <a href="/wiki/Siri" title="Siri">Siri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Google_Assistant" title="Google Assistant">Google Assistant</a> speak RP, and most TV and radio stations across the UK use this accent.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_dictionaries">In dictionaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: In dictionaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most English dictionaries published in Britain (including the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i>) now give phonetically transcribed RP pronunciations for all words. Pronunciation dictionaries represent a special class of dictionary giving a wide range of possible pronunciations; British pronunciation dictionaries are all based on RP, though not necessarily using that name. Daniel Jones transcribed RP pronunciations of a large number of words and names in the <i>English Pronouncing Dictionary</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1917_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1917-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Cambridge University Press continues to publish this title,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2011_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2011-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> as of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> edited by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Roach_(phonetician)" title="Peter Roach (phonetician)">Peter Roach</a>, the accent having been renamed "BBC Pronunciation". Two other pronunciation dictionaries are in common use: the <i>Longman Pronunciation Dictionary</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells2008_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells2008-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> compiled by <a href="/wiki/John_C._Wells" title="John C. Wells">John C. Wells</a> (using the name "Received Pronunciation"), and the <i>Oxford Dictionary of Pronunciation for Current English</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUptonKretzschmarKonopka2001_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUptonKretzschmarKonopka2001-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> compiled by <a href="/wiki/Clive_Upton" title="Clive Upton">Clive Upton</a>. This represents an accent named BR ("British English") – based on RP, but claimed to be representative of a wider group of speakers. An earlier pronunciation dictionary by <a href="/wiki/Jack_Windsor_Lewis" title="Jack Windsor Lewis">J. Windsor Lewis</a> gives both British and American pronunciations, using the terms General British (GB) for the former and General American (GA) for the latter.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Traditionally, Received Pronunciation was the "everyday speech in the families of <a href="/wiki/Southern_English_(England)" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern English (England)">Southern English</a> persons whose men-folk [had] been educated at the great public boarding-schools"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1917viii_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1917viii-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> and which conveyed no information about that speaker's region of origin before attending the school.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It is the business of educated people to speak so that no-one may be able to tell in what county their childhood was passed.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>A. Burrell, <i>Recitation. A Handbook for Teachers in Public Elementary School</i>, 1891</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In the 19th century, some British prime ministers still spoke with some regional features, such as <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> From the 1970s onwards, attitudes towards Received Pronunciation have been changing slowly. The BBC's use of Yorkshire-born <a href="/wiki/Wilfred_Pickles" title="Wilfred Pickles">Wilfred Pickles</a> during the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> (to distinguish BBC broadcasts from <a href="/wiki/German_propaganda" class="mw-redirect" title="German propaganda">German propaganda</a>) is an earlier example of the use of non-RP accents,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> but even then Pickles modified his speech towards RP when reading the news.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although admired in some circles, RP is disliked in others. It is common in parts of Britain to regard it as a south-eastern English accent rather than a non-regional one and as a symbol of the south-east's political power in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcArthur200243_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcArthur200243-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Based on a 1997 survey, Jane Stuart-Smith wrote, "RP has little status in <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a>, and is regarded with hostility in some quarters".<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> A 2007 survey found that residents of Scotland and Northern Ireland tend to dislike RP.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> It is shunned by some with <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">left-wing</a> political views, who may be proud of having an accent more typical of the <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working classes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcArthur200249_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcArthur200249-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Consonants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>Nasals and <a href="/wiki/Liquid_consonant" title="Liquid consonant">liquids</a> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/m/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/n/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ŋ/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l/</span>) may be <a href="/wiki/Syllabic_consonant" title="Syllabic consonant">syllabic</a> in <a href="/wiki/Unstressed_syllable" class="mw-redirect" title="Unstressed syllable">unstressed syllables</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach2004241_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach2004241-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The consonant in 'row', 'arrow' in RP is generally a <a href="/wiki/Postalveolar_approximant" class="mw-redirect" title="Postalveolar approximant">postalveolar approximant</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach2004241_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach2004241-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> which would normally be expressed with the sign <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɹ]</span> in the <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">International Phonetic Alphabet</a>, but the sign <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span> is nonetheless traditionally used for RP in most of the literature on the topic.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_plosive" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless plosive">Voiceless plosives</a> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/k/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/tʃ/</span>) are <a href="/wiki/Aspiration_(phonetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aspiration (phonetics)">aspirated</a> at the beginning of a syllable, unless a completely unstressed vowel follows. (For example, the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p/</span> is aspirated in "impasse", with primary stress on "-passe", but not "compass", where "-pass" has no stress.) Aspiration does not occur when <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/s/</span> precedes in the same syllable, as in "spot" or "stop". When a <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorant</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/w/</span>, or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/j/</span> follows, this aspiration is indicated by partial <a href="/wiki/Devoicing" class="mw-redirect" title="Devoicing">devoicing</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach2004240_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach2004240-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span> is a <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_non-sibilant_fricative" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless postalveolar non-sibilant fricative">fricative</a> when devoiced.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach2004241_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach2004241-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Syllable final <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/tʃ/</span>, and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/k/</span> may be either preceded by a <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a> (<a href="/wiki/Glottalization#Glottal_reinforcement" title="Glottalization">glottal reinforcement</a>) or, in the case of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/</span>, fully replaced by a glottal stop, especially before a <a href="/wiki/Syllabic_nasal" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllabic nasal">syllabic nasal</a> (<i>bitten</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈbɪʔn̩]</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach2004240_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach2004240-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGimson1970_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGimson1970-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The glottal stop may be realised as <a href="/wiki/Creaky_voice" title="Creaky voice">creaky voice</a>; thus, an alternative phonetic transcription of <i>attempt</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[əˈtʰemʔt]</span> could be <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[əˈtʰemm̰t]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach2004241_52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach2004241-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As in other varieties of English, voiced plosives (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/b/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/d/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɡ/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/dʒ/</span>) are partly or even fully devoiced at utterance boundaries or adjacent to <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_consonants" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless consonants">voiceless consonants</a>. The voicing distinction between voiced and voiceless sounds is reinforced by a number of other differences, with the result that the two of consonants can clearly be distinguished even in the presence of devoicing of voiced sounds:
</p>
<ol><li>Aspiration of voiceless consonants syllable-initially.</li>
<li>Glottal reinforcement of voiceless consonants syllable-finally.</li>
<li>Lengthening of vowels before voiced consonants.</li></ol>
<p>As a result, some authors prefer to use the terms "<a href="/wiki/Fortis_and_lenis" title="Fortis and lenis">fortis</a>" and "lenis" in place of "voiceless" and "voiced". However, the latter are traditional and in more frequent usage.
</p><p>The voiced dental fricative (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ð/</span>) is more often a weak <a href="/wiki/Dental_plosive" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental plosive">dental plosive</a>; the sequence <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/nð/</span> is often realised as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[n̪n̪]</span> (a long <a href="/wiki/Dental_nasal" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental nasal">dental nasal</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2009148–49_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2009148–49-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShockey200343–44_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShockey200343–44-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach2009112_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach2009112-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l/</span> has <a href="/wiki/Velarisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Velarisation">velarised</a> <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophone</a> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɫ]</span>) in the <a href="/wiki/Syllable_rime" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllable rime">syllable rhyme</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalleMohanan198565_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalleMohanan198565-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/h/</span> becomes voiced (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɦ]</span>) between <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">voiced</a> sounds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1967201_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1967201-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECruttenden2008204_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECruttenden2008204-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RP_English_monophthongs_chart.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/RP_English_monophthongs_chart.svg/220px-RP_English_monophthongs_chart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/RP_English_monophthongs_chart.svg/330px-RP_English_monophthongs_chart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/RP_English_monophthongs_chart.svg/440px-RP_English_monophthongs_chart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="980" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RP_English_monophthongs_chart.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Monophthongs of a fairly conservative variety of RP. From <a href="#CITEREFRoach2004">Roach (2004</a>, p.&#160;242)</div></div></div>

</p>

<p>Examples of <a href="/wiki/Long_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="Long vowel">long vowels</a>: <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/iː/</span> in <i>fl<b>ee</b>ce</i>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/uː/</span> in <i>g<b>oo</b>se</i>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/eə/</span> in <i>b<b>ear</b></i>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɜː/</span> in <i>n<b>ur</b>se</i> and <i>f<b>ur</b>ry</i>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔː/</span> in <i>n<b>or</b>th</i>, <i>f<b>or</b>ce</i> and <i>th<b>ou</b>ght</i>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɑː/</span> in <i>f<b>a</b>ther</i> and <i>st<b>ar</b>t</i>.
</p><p>The long mid front vowel <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">ɛː</a>&#93;</span> is transcribed with the traditional symbol &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">eə</span>&#x27e9; in this article. The predominant realisation in contemporary RP is <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">monophthongal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGimson2014118_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGimson2014118-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Long_and_short_vowels">Long and short vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Long and short vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>RP's long <a href="/wiki/High_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="High vowel">high vowels</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/iː/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/uː/</span> are slightly <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongised</a>, and are often narrowly transcribed in phonetic literature as diphthongs <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɪi]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʊu]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach200924_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach200924-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The terms "long" and "short" are relative to each other when applied to the vowel <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a> of RP. Vowels may be phonologically long or short (i.e. belong to the long or the short group of vowel phonemes) but their length is influenced by their context: in particular, they are shortened if a voiceless (<a href="/wiki/Fortis_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Fortis consonant">fortis</a>) consonant follows in the syllable, so that, for example, the vowel in 'bat' <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[bæʔt]</span> is shorter than the vowel in 'bad' <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[bæd]</span>. The process is known as <a href="/wiki/Clipping_(phonetics)" title="Clipping (phonetics)"><i>pre-fortis clipping</i></a>. Thus phonologically short vowels in one context can be phonetically <i>longer</i> than phonologically long vowels in another context.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach2004241_52-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach2004241-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> For example, the phonologically long vowel <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/iː/</span> in 'reach' <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/riːtʃ/</span> (which ends with a voiceless consonant) may be <i>shorter</i> than the phonologically short vowel <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɪ/</span> in the word 'ridge' <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/rɪdʒ/</span> (which ends with a voiced consonant). Wiik,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiik1965_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiik1965-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> cited in Cruttenden (2014), published durations of English vowels with a mean value of 17.2 csec. for short vowels before voiced consonants but a mean value of 16.5 csec for long vowels preceding voiceless consonants.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Natural_speech" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural speech">natural speech</a>, the plosives <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/d/</span> often have no audible release utterance-finally, and voiced consonants are partly or completely devoiced (as in <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[b̥æd̥]</span>); thus the perceptual distinction between pairs of words such as 'bad' and 'bat', or 'seed' and 'seat' rests mostly on vowel length (though the presence or absence of glottal reinforcement provides an additional cue).<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to such length distinctions, unstressed vowels are both shorter and more centralised than stressed ones. In unstressed syllables occurring before vowels and in final position, contrasts between long and short high vowels are neutralised and short <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[i]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[u]</span> occur (e.g. <i>happy</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈhæpi]</span>, <i>throughout</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[θɹuˈaʊʔt]</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach2004241,_243_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach2004241,_243-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The neutralisation is common throughout many English dialects, though the phonetic realisation of e.g. <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[i]</span> rather than <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɪ]</span> (a phenomenon called <a href="/wiki/Happy-tensing" class="mw-redirect" title="Happy-tensing"><i>happy</i>-tensing</a>) is not as universal.
</p><p>Unstressed vowels vary in quality:
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/i/</span> (as in <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r920966791">.mw-parser-output span.smallcaps{font-variant:small-caps}.mw-parser-output span.smallcaps-smaller{font-size:85%}</style><span class="smallcaps smallcaps-smaller">HAPP<span style="text-decoration: underline;">Y</span></span>) ranges from close front <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">i</a>&#93;</span> to close-mid retracted front <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Close-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid front unrounded vowel">e̠</a>&#93;</span>;<sup id="cite_ref-wellsxxv_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wellsxxv-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/u/</span> (as in <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791"/><span class="smallcaps smallcaps-smaller">INFL<span style="text-decoration: underline;">U</span>ENCE</span>) ranges from close advanced back <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">u̟</a>&#93;</span> to close-mid retracted central <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Close-mid_central_rounded_vowel" title="Close-mid central rounded vowel">ɵ̠</a>&#93;</span>;<sup id="cite_ref-wellsxxv_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wellsxxv-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> according to the phonetician <a href="/wiki/Jane_Setter" title="Jane Setter">Jane Setter</a>, the typical pronunciation of this vowel is a weakly rounded, mid-centralized <a href="/wiki/Close_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Close back unrounded vowel">close back unrounded vowel</a>, transcribed in the IPA as <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Near-close back rounded vowel">u̜̽</a>&#93;</span> or simply <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Near-close back rounded vowel">ʊ̜</a>&#93;</span>;<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ə/</span> (as in <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791"/><span class="smallcaps smallcaps-smaller">COMM<span style="text-decoration: underline;">A</span></span>) ranges from close-mid central <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Close-mid_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid central unrounded vowel">ɘ</a>&#93;</span> to open-mid central <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid central unrounded vowel">ɜ</a>&#93;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-wellsxxv_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wellsxxv-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diphthongs_and_triphthongs">Diphthongs and triphthongs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Diphthongs and triphthongs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RP_English_diphthongs_chart.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/RP_English_diphthongs_chart.svg/220px-RP_English_diphthongs_chart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/RP_English_diphthongs_chart.svg/330px-RP_English_diphthongs_chart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/RP_English_diphthongs_chart.svg/440px-RP_English_diphthongs_chart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="980" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RP_English_diphthongs_chart.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diphthongs of RP. From <a href="#CITEREFRoach2004">Roach (2004</a>, p.&#160;242)</div></div></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;">

<tbody><tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">Diphthong</a>
</th>

<p>The centring diphthongs are gradually being eliminated in RP. The vowel <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔə/</span> (as in "door", "boar") had largely merged with <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔː/</span> by the Second World War, and the vowel <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ʊə/</span> (as in "poor", "tour") has more recently merged with <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔː/</span> as well among most speakers,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERocaJohnson1999200_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERocaJohnson1999200-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> although the sound <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ʊə/</span> is still found in conservative speakers. See <a href="/wiki/Pour%E2%80%93poor_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Pour–poor merger">poor–pour merger</a>. The remaining centring glide <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɪə/</span> is increasingly pronounced as a monophthong <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɪː]</span>, although without merging with any existing vowels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach2004240_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach2004240-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The diphthong /əʊ/ is pronounced by some RP speakers in a noticeably different way when it occurs before /l/, if that consonant is <a href="/wiki/Syllable_final" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllable final">syllable-final</a> and not followed by a vowel (the context in which /l/ is pronounced as a "dark l"). The realization of /əʊ/ in this case begins with a more back, rounded and sometimes more open vowel quality; it may be transcribed as [ɔʊ] or [ɒʊ]. It is likely that the backness of the diphthong onset is the result of <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophonic</a> variation caused by the raising of the back of the tongue for the /l/. If the speaker has "l-vocalization" the /l/ is realized as a back rounded vowel, which again is likely to cause backing and rounding in a preceding vowel as <a href="/wiki/Coarticulation" title="Coarticulation">coarticulation</a> effects. This phenomenon has been discussed in several blogs by <a href="/wiki/John_C._Wells" title="John C. Wells">John C. Wells</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In the recording included in this article the phrase 'fold his cloak' contains examples of the /əʊ/ diphthong in the two different contexts. The onset of the pre-/l/ diphthong in 'fold' is slightly more back and rounded than that in 'cloak', though the allophonic transcription does not at present indicate this.
</p><p>RP also possesses the <a href="/wiki/Triphthong" title="Triphthong">triphthongs</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/aɪə/</span> as in <i>tire</i>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/aʊə/</span> as in <i>tower</i>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/əʊə/</span> as in <i>lower</i>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/eɪə/</span> as in <i>layer</i> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔɪə/</span> as in <i>loyal</i>. There are different possible realisations of these items: in slow, careful speech they may be pronounced as a two-syllable triphthong with three distinct vowel qualities in succession, or as a monosyllabic triphthong. In more casual speech the middle vowel may be considerably reduced, by a process known as <a href="/wiki/Smoothing_(phonetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Smoothing (phonetics)">smoothing</a>, and in an extreme form of this process the triphthong may even be reduced to a single vowel, though this is rare, and almost never found in the case of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔɪə/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach200918–19_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach200918–19-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In such a case the difference between <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/aʊə/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/aɪə/</span>, and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɑː/</span> in <i>tower</i>, <i>tire</i>, and <i>tar</i> may be <a href="/wiki/Archiphoneme" class="mw-redirect" title="Archiphoneme">neutralised</a> with all three units realised as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɑː]</span> or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[äː]</span>. This type of smoothing is known as the <a href="/wiki/English-language_vowel_changes_before_historic_/r/#Tower–tire,_tower–tar_and_tire–tar_mergers" title="English-language vowel changes before historic /r/"><i>tower</i>–<i>tire</i>, <i>tower</i>–<i>tar</i> and <i>tire</i>–<i>tar</i> mergers</a>.
</p>
