<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="BATH_vowel">BATH vowel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: BATH vowel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Trap%E2%80%93bath_split" title="Trap–bath split"><i>Trap–bath</i> split</a></div>
<p>There are differing opinions as to whether <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> in the <small>BATH</small> <a href="/wiki/Lexical_set" title="Lexical set">lexical set</a> can be considered RP. The pronunciations with <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɑː/</span> are invariably accepted as RP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982203_ff_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982203_ff-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The <i>English Pronouncing Dictionary</i> does not admit <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> in <small>BATH</small> words and the <i><a href="/wiki/Longman_Pronunciation_Dictionary" class="mw-redirect" title="Longman Pronunciation Dictionary">Longman Pronunciation Dictionary</a></i> lists them with a § marker of non-RP status.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> John Wells wrote in a blog entry on 16 March 2012 that when growing up in the north of England he used <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɑː/</span> in "bath" and "glass", and considers this the only acceptable phoneme in RP.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Others have argued that <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> is too categorical in the north of England to be excluded. Clive Upton believes that <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> in these words must be considered within RP and has called the opposing view "south-centric".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpton2004222–223_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpton2004222–223-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Upton's <i>Oxford Dictionary of Pronunciation for Current English</i> gives both variants for <small>BATH</small> words. A. F. Gupta's survey of mostly middle-class students found that <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> was used by almost everyone who was from clearly north of the <a href="/wiki/Isogloss" title="Isogloss">isogloss</a> for <small>BATH</small> words. She wrote, "There is no justification for the claims by Wells and Mugglestone that this is a sociolinguistic variable in the north, though it is a sociolinguistic variable on the areas on the border [the isogloss between north and south]".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGupta200525_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGupta200525-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In a study of speech in West Yorkshire, <a href="/wiki/KM_Petyt" class="mw-redirect" title="KM Petyt">K. M. Petyt</a> wrote that "the amount of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɑː/</span> usage is too low to correlate meaningfully with the usual factors", having found only two speakers (both having attended boarding schools in the south) who consistently used <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɑː/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetyt1985166–167_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetyt1985166–167-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Jack Windsor Lewis has noted that the Oxford Dictionary's position has changed several times on whether to include short <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> within its prescribed pronunciation.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The <i>BBC Pronouncing Dictionary of British Names</i> uses only <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɑː/</span>, but its author, Graham Pointon, has stated on his blog that he finds both variants to be acceptable in place names.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some research has concluded that many people in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">North of England</a> have a dislike of the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɑː/</span> vowel in <small>BATH</small> words. A. F. Gupta wrote, "Many of the northerners were noticeably hostile to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɡrɑːs/</span>, describing it as 'comical', 'snobbish', 'pompous' or even 'for morons'."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGupta200525_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGupta200525-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> On the subject, K. M. Petyt wrote that several respondents "positively said that they did not prefer the long-vowel form or that they really detested it or even that it was incorrect".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetyt1985286_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetyt1985286-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Mark Newbrook has assigned this phenomenon the name "conscious rejection", and has cited the <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791"/><span class="smallcaps smallcaps-smaller">BATH</span> vowel as "the main instance of conscious rejection of RP" in his research in West <a href="/wiki/Wirral_Peninsula" title="Wirral Peninsula">Wirral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewbrook1999101_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewbrook1999101-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="French_words">French words</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: French words">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>John Wells has argued that, as educated British speakers often attempt to pronounce French names in a French way, there is a case for including <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɒ̃/</span> (as in <i>bon</i>), and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ̃/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɜ̃:/</span> (as in <i>vingt-et-un</i>), as marginal members of the RP vowel system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells2008xxix_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells2008xxix-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> He also argues against including other French vowels on the grounds that very few British speakers succeed in distinguishing the vowels in <i>bon</i> and <i>banc</i>, or in <i>rue</i> and <i>roue</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells2008xxix_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells2008xxix-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_notation">Alternative notation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Alternative notation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Not all reference sources use the same system of transcription. In particular:
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> as in <i>trap</i> is also written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/a/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-blrpp_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blrpp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/e/</span> as in <i>dress</i> is also written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɛ/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-blrpp_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blrpp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmitt2007322–323_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmitt2007322–323-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ʌ/</span> as in <i>cup</i> is also written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɐ/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-blrpp_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blrpp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ʊ/</span> as in <i>foot</i> is also written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɵ/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-blrpp_87-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blrpp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɜː/</span> as in <i>nurse</i> is also written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/əː/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-blrpp_87-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blrpp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/aɪ/</span> as in <i>price</i> is also written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ʌɪ/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-blrpp_87-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blrpp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/aʊ/</span> as in <i>mouse</i> is also written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɑʊ/</span><sup id="cite_ref-blrpp_87-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blrpp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/eə/</span> as in <i>square</i> is also written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɛə/</span>, and is also sometimes treated as a long monophthong <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɛː/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-blrpp_87-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blrpp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/eɪ/</span> as in <i>face</i> is also written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɛɪ/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-blrpp_87-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blrpp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɪə/</span> as in <i>near</i> is also written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɪː/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-blrpp_87-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blrpp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/əʊ/</span> before <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l/</span> in a closed syllable as in <i>goal</i> is also written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔʊ/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-blrpp_87-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blrpp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/uː/</span> as in <i>goose</i> is also written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ʉː/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-blrpp_87-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blrpp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Most of these variants are used in the transcription devised by <a href="/wiki/Clive_Upton" title="Clive Upton">Clive Upton</a> for the <i><a href="/wiki/Shorter_Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Shorter Oxford English Dictionary">Shorter Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> (1993) and now used in many other <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">Oxford University Press</a> dictionaries.
</p><p>The linguist <a href="/wiki/Geoff_Lindsey" title="Geoff Lindsey">Geoff Lindsey</a> has argued that the system of transcription for RP has become outdated and has proposed a new system as a replacement.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_variation">Historical variation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Historical variation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like all accents, RP has changed with time. For example, sound recordings and films from the first half of the 20th century demonstrate that it was usual for speakers of RP to pronounce the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> sound, as in <i>land</i>, with a vowel close to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɛ]</span>, so that <i>land</i> would sound similar to a present-day pronunciation of <i>lend</i>. RP is sometimes known as <b>the Queen's English</b>, but recordings show that even <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> has changed her pronunciation over the past 50 years, no longer using an <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɛ]</span>-like vowel in words like <i>land</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RP_vowel_movement.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/RP_vowel_movement.png/350px-RP_vowel_movement.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/RP_vowel_movement.png/525px-RP_vowel_movement.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/RP_vowel_movement.png/700px-RP_vowel_movement.png 2x" data-file-width="1207" data-file-height="718" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RP_vowel_movement.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A comparison of the <a href="/wiki/Formant" title="Formant">formant</a> values of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/iː æ ɑː ɔː ʊ uː/</span> for older (black) and younger (light blue) RP speakers. From <a href="#CITEREFde_JongMcDougallHudsonNolan2007">de Jong et al. (2007</a>, p.&#160;1814)</div></div></div>
<p>Some changes in RP during the 20th century include:
</p>
<ul><li>Words such as <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791"/><span class="smallcaps smallcaps-smaller">CLOTH</span>, <i>gone</i>, <i>off</i>, <i>often</i> were pronounced with <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔː/</span> instead of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɒ/</span>, so that <i>often</i> and <i>orphan</i> were <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">homophones</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Lot%E2%80%93cloth_split" class="mw-redirect" title="Lot–cloth split"><i>lot</i>–<i>cloth</i> split</a>). The Queen still uses the older pronunciations,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> but it is rare to hear them on the BBC any more.</li>
<li>There was a <a href="/wiki/Horse%E2%80%93hoarse_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Horse–hoarse merger">distinction between <i>horse</i> and <i>hoarse</i></a> with an extra diphthong <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔə/</span> appearing in words like <i>hoarse</i>, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791"/><span class="smallcaps smallcaps-smaller">FORCE</span>, and <i>pour</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright19055_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright19055-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The symbols used are slightly different. Wright classifies the sound in <i>fall, law, saw' as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/oː/</span> and that in </i>more, soar,<i> etc. as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/oə/</span>.</i></li>
<li>The <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791"/><span class="smallcaps smallcaps-smaller">DRESS</span> vowel and the first element of the diphthong in <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791"/><span class="smallcaps smallcaps-smaller">FACE</span> was lowered from mid <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[e̞]</span> to open-mid <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɛ]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Any final y on a word is now represented as an <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/i/</span> – a symbol to cover either the traditional <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɪ/</span> or the more modern <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/iː/</span>, the latter of which has been common in the south of England for some time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgill199962_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgill199962-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Before the Second World War, the vowel of <i>cup</i> was a back vowel close to <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinal vowel">cardinal</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʌ]</span> but has since shifted forward to a central position so that <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɐ]</span> is more accurate; phonetic transcription of this vowel as &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʌ</span>&#x27e9; is common partly for historical reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERocaJohnson1999135,_186_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERocaJohnson1999135,_186-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In the 1960s, the transcription <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/əʊ/</span> started to be used for the <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791"/><span class="smallcaps smallcaps-smaller">GOAT</span> vowel instead of <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Jones_(phonetician)" title="Daniel Jones (phonetician)">Daniel Jones</a>'s <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/oʊ/</span>, reflecting a change in pronunciation since the beginning of the century.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The change in RP may be observed in the home of "BBC English". The BBC accent of the 1950s is distinctly different from today's: a news report from the 1950s is recognisable as such, and a mock-1950s BBC voice is used for comic effect in programmes wishing to satirise 1950s social attitudes such as the <a href="/wiki/Harry_Enfield" title="Harry Enfield">Harry Enfield Show</a> and its "Mr. Cholmondley-Warner" sketches.
</p><p>More recently, in speakers born between 1981 and 1993, the vowel <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɒ/</span> shifted up approaching <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">ɔ</a>&#93;</span> in quality.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWikström201345_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWikström201345-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The vowels <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ʊ/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/uː/</span> have undergone fronting and reduction in the amount of lip-rounding<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollinsMees2013207_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollinsMees2013207-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> (phonetically, this can be transcribed <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-close_central_rounded_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="Near-close central rounded vowel">ʊ̜̈</a>&#93;</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Close_central_rounded_vowel" title="Close central rounded vowel">ʉ̜ː</a>&#93;</span>, respectively), while <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> has become more open <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">a</a>&#93;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_JongMcDougallHudsonNolan20071814–1815_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_JongMcDougallHudsonNolan20071814–1815-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach2011_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach2011-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_other_varieties_of_English">Comparison with other varieties of English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Comparison with other varieties of English">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Like most other varieties of English outside Northern England, RP has undergone the <a href="/wiki/Foot%E2%80%93strut_split" class="mw-redirect" title="Foot–strut split"><i>foot</i>–<i>strut</i> split</a> (pairs <i>nut</i>/<i>put</i> differ).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982196_ff_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982196_ff-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>RP is a <a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">non-rhotic accent</a>, so <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span> does not occur unless followed immediately by a vowel (pairs such as <i>caught</i>/<i>court</i> and <i>formally</i>/<i>formerly</i> are homophones, save that <i>formerly</i> may be said with a hint of /r/ to help to differentiate it, particularly where stressed for reasons of emphasising past status e.g. "He was FORMERLY in charge here.").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells198276_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells198276-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Unlike most North American accents of English, RP has not undergone the <a href="/wiki/Mary%E2%80%93marry%E2%80%93merry_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary–marry–merry merger"><i>Mary</i>–<i>marry</i>–<i>merry</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Mirror%E2%80%93nearer_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Mirror–nearer merger"><i>nearer</i>–<i>mirror</i></a>, or <a href="/wiki/English-language_vowel_changes_before_historic_r#Hurry–furry_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="English-language vowel changes before historic r"><i>hurry</i>–<i>furry</i></a> mergers: all these words are distinct from each other.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982245_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982245-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Unlike many North American accents, RP has not undergone the <a href="/wiki/Father%E2%80%93bother_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Father–bother merger"><i>father</i>–<i>bother</i></a> or <a href="/wiki/Cot%E2%80%93caught_merger" title="Cot–caught merger"><i>cot</i>–<i>caught</i> mergers</a>.</li>
<li>RP does not have <a href="/wiki/English_consonant-cluster_reductions#Yod-dropping" class="mw-redirect" title="English consonant-cluster reductions">yod-dropping</a> after <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/n/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/d/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/z/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/θ/</span>, but most speakers of RP variably or consistently <i>yod</i>-drop after <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/s/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l/</span> &#8212; <i>new</i>, <i>tune</i>, <i>dune</i>, <i>resume</i> and <i>enthusiasm</i> are pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/njuː/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/tjuːn/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/djuːn/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/rɪˈzjuːm/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɪnˈθjuːziæzm/</span> rather than <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/nuː/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/tuːn/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/duːn/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/rɪˈzuːm/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɪnˈθuːziæzm/</span>. This contrasts with many <a href="/wiki/East_Anglian_English" title="East Anglian English">East Anglian</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_English" title="East Midlands English">East Midland</a> varieties of <a href="/wiki/English_language_in_England" title="English language in England">English language in England</a> and with many forms of <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a>, including <a href="/wiki/General_American" class="mw-redirect" title="General American">General American</a>. Hence also <i>pursuit</i> is commonly heard with <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/j/</span> and <i>revolutionary</i> less so but more commonly than <i>evolution</i>. For a subset of these, a yod has been lost over time: for example, in all of the words beginning <i>suit</i>, however the yod is sometimes deliberately reinserted in historical or stressed contexts such as "a suit in chancery" or "suitable for an aristocrat".</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Flapping" title="Flapping">flapped</a> variant of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/d/</span> (as in much of the West Country, Ulster, most North American varieties including General American, Australian English, and the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Coloured" class="mw-redirect" title="Cape Coloured">Cape Coloured</a> dialect of South Africa) is not used very often.</li>
<li>RP has undergone <a href="/wiki/Wine%E2%80%93whine_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Wine–whine merger"><i>wine</i>–<i>whine</i> merger</a> (so the sequence <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/hw/</span> is not present except among those who have acquired this distinction as the result of speech training).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982228_ff_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982228_ff-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy_of_Dramatic_Art" title="Royal Academy of Dramatic Art">Royal Academy of Dramatic Art</a>, based in London, still teaches these two sounds for international breadth as distinct phonemes. They are also distinct from one another in most of Scotland and Ireland, in the northeast of England, and in the southeastern United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982228_ff_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982228_ff-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Unlike some other varieties of <a href="/wiki/English_language_in_England" title="English language in England">English language in England</a>, there is no <a href="/wiki/H-dropping" title="H-dropping"><i>h</i>-dropping</a> in words like <i>head</i> or <i>horse</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982253_ff_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982253_ff-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> As shown in the spoken specimen below, in hurried phrases such as "as hard as he could" h-dropping commonly applies to the word <i>he</i>.</li>
<li>Unlike most <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Hemisphere English">Southern Hemisphere English</a> accents, RP has not undergone the <a href="/wiki/Weak-vowel_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Weak-vowel merger">weak-vowel merger</a>, meaning that pairs such as <i>Lenin</i>/<i>Lennon</i> are distinct.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982167_ff_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982167_ff-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In traditional RP <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɾ]</span> is an <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophone</a> of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span> (it is used intervocalically, after <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/θ</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ð/</span> and sometimes even after <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/b</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɡ/</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWise1957_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWise1957-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECruttenden2008221_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECruttenden2008221-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservative_RP">Conservative RP</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Conservative RP">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Received+Pronunciation%22">"Received Pronunciation"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Received+Pronunciation%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Received+Pronunciation%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Received+Pronunciation%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Received+Pronunciation%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Received+Pronunciation%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2018</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Conservative Received Pronunciation is a conservative standard of pronunciation of <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a>. Formerly the prestige model of pronunciation, it has declined in favour of other, less-conservative dialects, primarily Contemporary Received Pronunciation (Contemporary RP) also known as <i>Modern RP</i>. Conservative RP is the standard adhered to in the First and Second Editions of the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i>, which, starting with the Third edition, has been modelled on Contemporary RP.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Other terms for Conservative RP are <i>Traditional RP</i> and <i>Upper RP</i> (the latter in reference to the association of the standard to the upper class and aristocracy). Notable speakers of Conservative RP include <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> and other older members of the <a href="/wiki/British_Royal_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="British Royal Family">British Royal Family</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Winston_Churchill" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Winston Churchill">Sir Winston Churchill</a> and commentators of <a href="/wiki/Path%C3%A9_News" title="Pathé News">Pathé News</a> and, prior to the 1960s, the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>.
</p><p>The phonological features of Conservative RP which are distinct from Contemporary RP, include:
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels_and_diphthongs">Vowels and diphthongs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Vowels and diphthongs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Happy_tensing" class="mw-redirect" title="Happy tensing">Happy tensing</a>: this feature concerns the vowel at the end of words ending in &#x27e8;y&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;ie&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;ee&#x27e9;, etc., which is normally transcribed with the symbol &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">i</span>&#x27e9;. In Conservative RP, this vowel can be assigned to the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɪ/</span> phoneme, whereas in Contemporary RP it can be assigned to the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/iː/</span> phoneme, as it is more tense.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The phonetic realization of the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/e/</span> phoneme is more close (<span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Close-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid front unrounded vowel">e</a>&#93;</span>) than in Contemporary RP, in which it is more open <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">ɛ</a>&#93;</span>. The more closed realization is also found in Australia, New Zealand and South Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Similarly, the quality of the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> phoneme is <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-open front unrounded vowel">æ</a>&#93;</span>, akin to <a href="/wiki/General_American" class="mw-redirect" title="General American">General American</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Australian" class="mw-redirect" title="General Australian">General Australian</a>. However, the majority of Contemporary RP speakers realize this vowel as fully open <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">a</a>&#93;</span>, as do speakers from Northern England and Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The quality of the <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791"/><span class="smallcaps smallcaps-smaller">NURSE</span> vowel <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɜː/</span> is realised as <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-open_central_vowel" title="Near-open central vowel">ɐː</a>&#93;</span> by some conservative speakers, and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid central unrounded vowel">ɜː</a> ~ <a href="/wiki/Mid_central_vowel" title="Mid central vowel">əː</a>]</span> by others. In Contemporary RP, the <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-open_central_vowel" title="Near-open central vowel">ɐː</a>&#93;</span> realization is not heard.</li>
<li>Many terms have <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔː/</span> in Conservative RP, yet <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɒ/</span> in the speech of Contemporary RP speakers, including <i>cross</i>, <i>often</i>, <i>cloth</i>, <i>salt</i>, <i>because</i>, <i>gone</i>,  etc. Similarly, the term <i>mass</i> (with reference to the Catholic ritual) may be pronounced as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/mɑːs/</span> by conservative speakers, with <i>data</i> also possessing this vowel, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ˈdɑːtə/</span>. For Contemporary RP speakers, the former tends to have the short <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> vowel, whereas the latter has a diphthong <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/eɪ/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In some cases, where Contemporary RP has the schwa <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ə/</span>, Conservative RP preserves <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɪ/</span>, for instance, the final vowel in the following: <i>devil</i>, <i>kindness</i>, <i>witness</i>, <i>private</i>, <i>toilet</i>, <i>fortunate</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_111-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Contemporary RP speakers realize <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/əʊ/</span> as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɔʊ]</span> before the dark l (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɫ]</span>), so that <i>goal</i> has a different vowel from <i>goat</i>. This allophone is not used by conservative speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_111-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Two diphthongs which exist in Conservative RP may not in Contemporary RP. The first has disappeared in the speech of all but the most conservative British speakers and some speakers of Southern American English, the <a href="/wiki/English-language_vowel_changes_before_historic_/r/" title="English-language vowel changes before historic /r/">hoarse-horse distinction</a>. For speakers who differentiate, <i>hoarse</i> is realised as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/hɔəs/</span> and <i>horse</i> is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/hɔːs/</span>. The vowel in words such as <i>tour</i>, <i>moor</i>, <i>sure</i> is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ʊə/</span> for all Conservative RP speakers, but has merged with <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔː/</span> for many Contemporary speakers. Taking the two mergers into account, results in a number of three-way mergers, which were hitherto distinct, such as <i>poor</i>, <i>paw</i> and <i>pore</i> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/pʊə/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/pɔː/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/pɔə/</span>) all becoming <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/pɔː/</span>.</li>
<li>The <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɛə/</span> phoneme (as in <i>fair</i>, <i>care</i>, <i>there</i>) is realized as a true centring diphthong <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɛə]</span> in the conservative variety, whereas speakers of Contemporary RP tend to realise it as a long monophthong <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">ɛː</a>&#93;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_111-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/eɪ/</span> phoneme has a somewhat different starting point in the conservative variety, namely <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[eɪ]</span>. For Contemporary speakers, this vowel tends to be realised as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɛɪ]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_111-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants_2">Consonants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Consonants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Unlike with vowels and diphthongs, consonantal phonemes have not undergone change, with one exception. For speakers of Conservative Received Pronunciation in the late 19th century, it was standard for the consonant combination &#x27e8;wh&#x27e9; to be realised as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ʍ/</span> (also transcribed <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/hw/</span>), as can still be heard in the 21st century in the speech of many speakers in Ireland and Scotland and a large minority in the Southern United States. Since the beginning of the 20th century, however, the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ʍ/</span> phoneme ceased to be a feature of Conservative RP, except by the most precise speakers who have learnt to differentiate, meaning it has ceased to be a native feature of English outside Ireland, Scotland (and some bordering areas of England) and the Southern United States.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spoken_specimen">Spoken specimen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Spoken specimen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">

<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>

<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>

<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_the_International_Phonetic_Association" title="Journal of the International Phonetic Association">Journal of the International Phonetic Association</a></i> regularly publishes "Illustrations of the IPA" which present an outline of the phonetics of a particular language or accent. It is usual to base the description on a recording of the traditional story of <a href="/wiki/The_North_Wind_and_the_Sun" title="The North Wind and the Sun">the North Wind and the Sun</a>. There is an IPA illustration of British English (Received Pronunciation).
</p><p>The speaker (female) is described as having been born in 1953, and educated at <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a>. To accompany the recording there are three transcriptions: orthographic, phonemic and allophonic.
</p>
<blockquote>
<p><b>Phonemic</b>
</p><p><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ðə ˈnɔːθ ˈwɪnd ən ðə ˈsʌn wə dɪˈspjuːtɪŋ ˈwɪtʃ wəz ðə ˈstrɒŋɡə, wen ə ˈtrævl̩ə ˌkeɪm əˌlɒŋ ˈræpt ɪn ə ˈwɔːm ˈkləʊk. ðeɪ əˈɡriːd ðət ðə ˈwʌn hu ˈfɜːst səkˈsiːdɪd ɪn ˈmeɪkɪŋ ðə ˈtrævlə ˌteɪk hɪz ˈkləʊk ɒf ʃʊd bi kənˌsɪdəd ˈstrɒŋɡə ðən ði ˈʌðə. ˈðen ðə ˌnɔːθ wɪnd ˈbluː əz ˈhɑːd əz i ˈkʊd, bət ðə ˈmɔː hi ˈbluː ðə ˌmɔː ˈkləʊsli dɪd ðə ˈtrævlə ˈfəʊld hɪz ˌkləʊk əˈraʊnd hɪm, ænd ət ˈlɑːst ðə ˈnɔ:θ wɪnd ˌɡeɪv ˈʌp ði əˈtempt. ˈðen ðə ˈsʌn ˌʃɒn aʊt ˈwɔːmli, ænd əˈmiːdiətli ðə ˈtrævlə ˈtʊk ɒf ɪz ˈkləʊk. n̩ ˌsəʊ ðə ˈnɔːθ ˈwɪn wəz əˈblaɪdʒd tʊ kənˈfes ðət ðə ˈsʌn wəz ðə ˈstrɒŋɡr̩ əv ðə ˈtuː.</span>
</p><p><b>Allophonic</b>
</p><p><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ðə ˈnɔːθ ˈw̥ɪnd ən̪n̪ə ˈsʌn wə dɪˈspj̊u̟ːtɪŋ ˈwɪʔtʃ wəz ðə ˈstɹ̥ɒŋɡə, wen ə ˈtɹ̥ævl̩ə ˌkʰeɪm əˌlɒŋ ˈɹæptʰ ɪn ə ˈwɔːm ˈkl̥əʊkˣ. ðeɪ əˈɡɹ̥iːd̥ ð̥əʔ ðə ˈwʌn ɦu ˈfɜːs səkˈsiːdɪd ɪmˈmeɪxɪŋ ðə ˈtɹ̥ævlə ˌtʰeɪk̟x̟ɪs ˈkl̥əʊk ɒf ʃʊbbi kʰənˌsɪdəd̥ ˈstɹɒŋɡə ð̥ən̪n̪i ˈʌðə. ˈðen̪n̪ə ˌnɔːθ w̥ɪnd ˈbluː əz̥ ˈhɑːd̥ əs i ˈkʊd, bət̬ ð̥ə ˈmɔː hi ˈblu̟ː ðə ˌmɔ ˈkl̥əʊsl̥i d̥ɨd ð̥ə ˈtɹ̥æv̥lə ˈfəʊld̥ hɪz̥ ˌkl̥əʊkʰ əˈɹaʊnd hɪm, ænd ət ˈl̥ɑːst ð̥ə ˈnɔ:θ w̥ɪnd ˌɡ̊eɪv̥ ˈʌp ði̥ əˈtʰemʔt. ˈðen̪n̪ə ˈsʌn ˌʃɒn aʊt ˈwɔːmli, ænd əˈmiːdiətl̥i ð̥ə ˈtɹ̥ævlə ˈtʰʊk ɒf ɪz̥ ˈkl̥əʊkˣ. n̩ ˌsəʊ ðə ˈnɔːθ ˈw̥ɪn wəz̥ əˈblaɪdʒ̊ tʰɵ kʰənˈfes ð̥əʔ ð̥ə ˈsʌn wəz̥z̥ə ˈstɹ̥ɒŋɡɹ̩ əv̥ ð̥ə ˈtʰu̟ː.</span>
</p><p><b>Orthographic</b>
</p><p>The North Wind and the Sun were disputing which was the stronger, when a traveller came along wrapped in a warm cloak. They agreed that the one who first succeeded in making the traveller take his cloak off should be considered stronger than the other. Then the North Wind blew as hard as he could, but the more he blew the more closely did the traveller fold his cloak around him, and at last the North Wind gave up the attempt. Then the Sun shone out warmly, and immediately the traveller took off his cloak. And so the North Wind was obliged to confess that the Sun was the stronger of the two.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach2004_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach2004-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_speakers">Notable speakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Notable speakers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The following people have been described as RP speakers:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/David_Attenborough" title="David Attenborough">David Attenborough</a>, broadcaster and naturalist<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/British_Royal_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="British Royal Family">British Royal Family</a><sup id="cite_ref-Wells_2011_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wells_2011-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup></li>

<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Wells, John (27 January 1994). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.phon.ucl.ac.uk/home/wells/rphappened.htm">"Whatever happened to Received Pronunciation?"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">24 August</span> 2011</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Whatever+happened+to+Received+Pronunciation%3F&amp;rft.date=1994-01-27&amp;rft.aulast=Wells&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.phon.ucl.ac.uk%2Fhome%2Fwells%2Frphappened.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReceived+Pronunciation" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEWikström201345-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWikström201345_98-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWikström2013">Wikström (2013)</a>, p.&#160;45. "It seems to be the case that younger RP or near-RP speakers typically use a closer quality, possibly approaching Cardinal 6 considering that the quality appears to be roughly intermediate between that used by older speakers for the LOT vowel and that used for the THOUGHT vowel, while older speakers use a more open quality, between Cardinal Vowels 13 and 6."</span>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="External_links">External links</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Received_Pronunciation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: External links">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.bbc.co.uk/radio4/routesofenglish/storysofar/posh.shtml">BBC page on Upper RP as spoken by the English upper-classes</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.bl.uk/learning/langlit/sounds/case-studies/received-pronunciation/">Sounds Familiar?</a>&#160;&#8211;&#32;Listen to examples of received pronunciation on the British Library's 'Sounds Familiar' website</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.lel.ed.ac.uk/research/gsound/Eng/Direct/Englishes/SglLgRPStandard.htm">'Hover &amp; Hear' R.P.</a>, and compare it with other accents from the UK and around the World.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.phon.ucl.ac.uk/home/wells/rphappened.htm">Whatever happened to Received Pronunciation?</a> – An article by the phonetician <a href="/wiki/John_C._Wells" title="John C. Wells">J. C. Wells</a> about received pronunciation</li></ul>
